0393338827|5.0|THE BIG SHORT by Michael Lewis is a romp through the labyrinth world of shadowy players trying to make money from the subprime mortgage crisis.<br /><br />Lewis's narrative includes key players on both sides of the subprime mess, those who own the debt and those who are hoping to \\"short\\" it, or profit from its decline in value. From Goldman Sachs and the big wirehouse firms (Bear Stearns, Merrill, Lehman, Morgan Stanley) to a lonely solo money manager to a group of young investors, he traces the murky trail of a handful of people who would profit handsomely from the largest failures in modern finance. There weren't many. Nearly all of the \\"experts\\" were blind (ignorantly or willfully) to the debacle about to engulf the country.<br /><br />It is astonishing that so many highly-paid, highly-educated financial experts missed the unmistakeable.  Like lemmings off the proverbial cliff, all the major players fell into disaster. Some would survive, though wounded (Morgan, DeutscheBank, Merrill) but others would fail altogether (Countrywide,  Bear Stearns, Lehman).<br /><br />No one tweaks the nose of Wall Street better than Michael Lewis. His savvy and smarts are evident, yet so are his wit and irreverence for the hallowed halls of Wall Street. This truly was a David and Goliath story - and it is still playing out as millions of bad home loans and foreclosed properties are deleveraged throughout  the financial system.<br /><br />For a highly-readable, delicious tale about the mighty and powerful  as well as a certain courage and pluck by smaller guys willing to take on the system...and win....I highly recommend this book.

B005CAAU76|5.0|From reading other reviews, it seems the perception by some is that this album was a bit of a letdown upon its initial release in 1978, but I certainly never saw it that way.  I'll get some disagreement on this I'm sure, but to me this is one of Queen's top 2 or 3 albums ever and the last truly great Queen album to feature all the elements of the classic Queen sound.  For those not familiar with it, I'll cover the main album first.  If you're more interested in just the remastering effort and/or bonus material, skip towards the end of the review where that's covered separately.<br /><br />As for the album itself, first, the hit single songs most listeners will already know:  Fat Bottomed Girls & Bicycle Race.  These are not personal favorites of mine and actually two of the weaker tracks on the album to me.  They're not at all bad, but I think the reason some may feel this album was something of a letdown is that they are likely judging the album on the strength of the \\"hits\\" it had (or didn't have).  If so, I would say they are missing out on some of Queen's best work because while these are probably not 2 of Queen's stronger singles, the deeper cuts on this album are all superb.<br /><br />What constitutes Queen's \\"best work\\" is of course up to the individual, but to me, it's diversity of material with consistent quality and in that regard `Jazz' delivers like no other Queen album except for perhaps \\"A Night at the Opera\\". Whereas most opening tracks on previous Queen albums were straight rockers like Brighton Rock, Tie Your Mother Down, We Will Rock You, etc.,  \\"Jazz\\" opens with the middle-Eastern flair of \\"Mustapha\\", signaling that Queen are going to take a few chances on this one....  I have to admit it threw me at first but over time, I've grown to really love this unique track.  Freddie really shines and it does rock out plenty as it progresses.  Two of the next three songs are the singles - `Fat Bottomed Girls' & `Bicycle Race' - again, not my favorites, butnot bad songs at all.  In between is a great ballad by Freddie Mercury (\\"Jealousy\\") that's always been a favorite of mine.  A slightly odd order to start the album, but it flows, is still plenty good and most importantly, only gets better.<br /><br />Next up is one of the few (only?) really rocking tunes John Deacon wrote for Queen, \\"If You Can't Beat Them\\".  Excellent lyrics, the trademark sound from Brian May and Queen's one-of-a-kind backup vocals to fill it out.  This fades out and brings us to \\"Let Me Entertain You\\", a perfect statement rocker from Freddie Mercury, though strong as the guitar riff for this song is, you might think Brian May had written it.  And the lyrics sum up Mercury's approach to his role in Queen most fittingly:<br /><br />I've come here to sell you my body<br />I can show you some good merchandise<br />I'll pull you and I'll pill you<br />I'll Cruella-Daville you<br />To thrill you I'll use any device<br /><br />A superb ending to Side 1, though again, slightly odd placement since this was used as an opening tone-setter for Queen's live show for the next several years, yet closes Side 1 here.<br /><br />Side 2 is really where this album shines though: 7 songs, all solid, and surprisingly, only 1 composed by Freddie Mercury (although to me, Brian May's \\"Dreamer's Ball\\" always seemed like more of a Freddie song, which helps keep the side more balanced).  Side 2 opens right where side 1 left off, with a great rocking tune, this one by Brian May (\\"Dead on Time\\").  Excellent guitar work and spot-on lyrics about the frantic pace of life that Queen was dealing with more and more by 1978 after years of platinum albums and sold-out world tours.  And the lyrics match the fast-pace nature of the song.  Next up is the wonderful John Deacon ballad, \\"In Only Seven Days\\" about a week-long romance while on vacation which is perfectly understated musically and superbly sung by Freddie Mercury.<br /><br />Next up is the previously mentioned \\"Dreamer's Ball\\", a playful little number Queen always had fun with on stage and that harkens back to songs like \\"Seaside Rendezvous\\" and \\"Who Needs You\\" from previous albums.  So.... not to be taken too seriously, but still a wonderful track with some excellent guitar work from Brian May in a slightly different setting.  This is followed by the first of two songs by drummer Roger Taylor, the funky-disco beat of \\"Fun It\\" which is augmented by Brian May's guitar to still give it that signature Queen sound.  Lead vocals are shared by Taylor and Mercury on this precursor to \\"Another One Bites The Dust\\", though this is a little more rocking than \\"Dust\\" as it doesn't stray quite as far into the funk/disco genre.<br /><br />The side winds down with another track from Brian May, this one a pensive ballad about separation and having to leave the ones you love (\\"Leaving Home Ain't Easy\\").  For this one, Brian takes lead vocals himself and while his voice has always seemed very limited to me, it's very effective with the right material and shines on this track.  This song provides yet another touch to give Queen the diversity that made them shine so brightly on this album.<br /><br />Next up is another single from the album, Freddie's lone contribution to side 2, \\"Don't Stop Me Now\\".  It did not fare particularly well in the U.S. but did reach the top 10 in the UK.  It's perhaps a bit over the top for U.S. audiences but undeniably catchy and I remember latching onto this song very early after I first purchased this album back in 1978.  It was also used to great effect in the motion picture \\"Shaun of the Dead\\" during a bar-fight scene.<br /><br />Closing the side and the album is the aptly titled \\"More of That Jazz,\\" a down-tempo funky little rocker from Roger Taylor.  The lyrics center on Taylor's bitter comments about current society and the way rock and roll is disrespected.  Taylor plays most of the instruments himself and sings all vocals.  A superb track unlike anything else on the album, but then that could be said for most of the tracks on this excellent Queen release.  The outro contains a summation of the album by way of short clips from many songs on the album including \\"Dead on Time\\", \\"Bicycle Race\\", \\"Mustapha\\", \\"If You Can't Beat Them\\", \\"Fun It\\", and \\"Fat Bottomed Girls\\", making it the perfect album closer.<br /><br />All told, I love every single track on this album except (ironically) the two singles they aimed at mass audiences, \\"Fat Bottomed Girls\\" and \\"Bicycle Race\\".  And those are far from bad songs, but not as strong to me as the rest of the album.  Not bad at all though which makes the complete \\"Jazz\\" package one of Queen's very best to this reviewer.  After \\"Jazz\\", Queen would intentionally water down the diversity and never be quite this adventurous again in an effort to reach new fans, which they would successfully do on their next release, 1980's \\"The Game\\".  And that's a fine album too, but compared to \\"Jazz\\" is a pretty \\"safe\\" album and let's face it:  what made Queen so great in the 70's is that they were anything BUT \\"safe\\".  So I consider \\"Jazz\\" to be the last truly classic Queen album that featured them at their best and demonstrating all their strengths in full.  FIVE STARS for the Jazz album.<br /><br />REMASTERING and BONUS TRACKS:<br /><br />And now a few words on the REMASTERING effort and the BONUS TRACKS:  The first Queen 2011 remasters I bought were the first five albums, which I wanted mainly for the bonus material.  I did evaluate the remastering job both with my naked ears and then by taking a couple of tracks from Sheer Heart Attack and Queen I and looking at the wav files on my computer, comparing the same songs from my 1991 remasters to those on the 2011 remasters.  On the ear test, I noticed no real discernable difference in the volume or quality.  It sounded good, but then so did my 1991 remasters....  In looking at the wav files, the 1991 files appeared identical to those of the 2011 tracks.  So the goodnews is that these remasters weren't just volume-adjusted to get these cranked way up loud (which is all too common and just results in clipping and reduced sound quality/clarity).  The bad news is that these new re-masters also don't really sound any better than the previous 1991 remasters either.<br /><br />So to me it appears the only reason to get the remasters would be the bonus material, which is also a mixed bag.  First, they have for some reason decided to put the bonus material on a second disc when it would easily fit on the main disc.  I guess having it on the 2nd disc (they feel) adds to the \\"specialness\\" of it, but it also adds to the COST, so I don't get it.  The other mini-complaint I have is that there are live tracks on some albums (including this one) that are already out there on readily available live albums such as \\"Queen Rock Montreal\\" or \\"On Fire At The Bowl\\" (the live version of \\"Let Me Entertain You\\" here is one such track and comes from the \\"Montreal\\" live CD).  What's the point there?  Particularly since it's not a very good version:  it's an up-tempo number already and Queen play it so fast here that they seem in race to get to the finish.  The version on 1979's \\"Live Killers\\" is much better...<br /><br />Then we have versions of some songs that are the same as what's on the main album, but with the vocals removed (\\"Bicycle Race\\").  Again, I ask:  what's the point of that?  I guess karaoke fans might like that but to me it's a waste of space.  The \\"Fat Bottomed Girls\\" single version is very similar to the album version but I guess is ok. Hardly a revelation though. That leaves 2 bonus tracks of interest:  the version of \\"Don't' Stop Me Now\\" with the \\"long lost guitars\\" features Brian May noodling around during the piano intro and a slightly different guitar solo, and it's somewhat interesting to hear what Queen considered doing differently with this song, but it's not drastically different nor is it an improvement and I can't see anyonepreferring this version to the version that ended up on the album, so again, not a particularly wortwhile bonus track to me.<br /><br />Thankfully the very last bonus track really delivers:  the early acoustic take of \\"Dreamer's Ball\\" is in fact a studio run through never intended for release, just the skeleton of the song delivered very informally by the band (minus John Deacon) with Roger on brushes, Brian on acoustic and of course Freddie delivering the vocal.  It's a light-hearted run-through primarily to flush out the arrangement and get them familiar with the parts they potentially intended to play on the formal recording.  Here though, it sounds closer to a smoky blues-jazz club at 1:00 a.m. (something like \\"My Melancholy Blues\\" from their \\"News of the World\\" album) than the final version that ended up on the \\"Jazz\\" album with electric guitars, etc.  This is a great song and a nice alternate version to be enjoyed and to me, really rescues the bonus material disc.<br /><br />So, my grades:<br /><br />THE JAZZ ALBUM:  5 stars, a near flawless album.  Definitely one of Queen's very best!<br /><br />REMASTERING GRADE:  2.5 stars (avg because there's really no discernable difference, but at least they didn't ruin it)<br /><br />BONUS TRACKS: 2 stars because only 1 of the 5 tracks is really worthwhile here (\\"Dreamer's Ball\\").  And if you don't care much for that song, I dare say there's nothing of interest on this bonus disc and considering the so-so remastering, you might do just as well (and save some $) by picking up the 1991 version of \\"Jazz\\".

B006UG909U|4.0|On A Different Kind of Truth, Van Halen is reborn. Upon hearing the first single \\"Tattoo\\", I figured this new album would be good but still make you long for the old days. But the boys got their mojo back big time. No ballads, no going through the motions, this is straight out in your face rock n' roll. The production is also very well done: it's loud while not being overly compressed. Upon hearing these songs for the first time, you already know these will sound amazing live. The performances are full of vigor as David Lee Roth's charisma is on full display while Eddie Van Halen's guitar work is that of a man possessed. All of the songs range from good to great with the aforementioned and decent \\"Tattoo\\" being perhaps the weakest track. \\"You and Your Blues\\", \\"Blood and Fire\\", and \\"Stay Frosty\\" have catchy riffs and choruses and would be excellent choices as the next singles. \\"Stay Frosty\\", in particular, is very good as its acoustic intro and David's delivery recalls the vibe of \\"Take Your Whiskey Home\\" and \\"Ice Cream Man\\" before the drums kick in. \\"China Town\\", \\"As Is\\", and \\"Honeybabysweetie Doll\\" are among the heaviest songs they've ever recorded and Eddie's solos are really menacing on these tunes.  Other spirited tracks here include \\"Big River\\", \\"Outta Space\\", and \\"The Trouble with Never\\". Easily their best album since 1984 and better than any of the albums with Sammy Hagar or Gary Cherone, A Different Kind Of Truth is one of the strongest hard rock albums of recent years, certainly nothing you'd expect with the three core guys in the band pushing 60. Highly recommended.

B0067QPVD2|1.0|I am all for adding to a mythos but that doesn't mean it is always a good thing (no pun intended).  How can one rate this film without looking at the John Carpenter version? Since it is meant as a direct prequel, I would argue that you can't. So, from an the point of view of one of the members of the expedition (which was John Carpenter's intent)let's give an overview of that version:  An antarctic research crew encounters a being which can mimic anyone or anything it absorbs (and summarily duplicates). It turns out that this being is extraterrestrial in origin and is both cunning and pretty successful as an ambush predator, not to mention very intelligent. It is the ultimate ninja (not the video game kind) as it patiently waits, bides its time and methodically begins taking over whomever it can. It acts with intelligence and foresight by absorbing/assuming individuals as it can and only ONLY acting in direct opposition when cornered, exposed or weak. But even then it has a back-up plan. When it ambushed Bennings and was discovered part way through, it (as the Thing was also at least Palmer and Norris by this time) acted against itself to protect the greater whole. Now none of this is to say the Thing didn't make some mistakes. It did attack the dogs under the assumption that it would be able to successfully absorb all of them. The noise worked against it in that case. The point being is the Thing acted with intelligence as a thinking being would do and was under the same (or very similar) physical limitations as terrestrial creatures. Even the finale blatantly suggested that freezing was now its plan as a rescue team would likely show up eventually taking it to more civilized areas.  Now here comes The Thing (2011), the prequel the the 1982 John Carpenter version which deals with the Norwegian Camp's experiences. Did not have any problem with any of the actors. Felt Mary Elizabeth Winstead was great for her part. The film itself? Hmm, let's see, early in the film they find the block of ice containing the Thing. It then breaks out of the ice? Really? So it freezes because it can't do anything else but then breaks out of the ice because, what, it didn't really need to allow itself to freeze in the first place. I am confused at this point. As the film progresses, one of the Things is now in a helicopter being taken away from the Norwegian camp which would eventually lead to more civilized areas (**Edit: I stand corrected, the helicopter was called back to the base before the Thing attacked. I still feel the attack in the air goes against how the Thing normally acted as both patient hunter and intelligent creature). So instead of biding its time it attacks the helicopter crew and allowing itself to crash and be trapped back at the Norwegian camp? Really? I guess the whole intelligent being idea was totally scrapped. I did like the idea that it can't replicate artificial parts (i.e. fillings and knees replacements) but then it decides to ambush our Hero Mary Elizabeth Winstead in the most profoundly clumsy and noisy way possible? Really? Which of course does not succeed and puts everyone that wasn't already on edge, in total Red Alert mode. I'm sorry, the film came off the rails pretty quick in Act II. What followed was just a loud, noisy action film with plenty of monster hi-jinx and scene match-ups with the 1982 version of The Thing when they travel to the Norwegian camp.  Ultimately a failure in my opinion, due to the plot, writing or whatever mechanism completely stripped our villain (i.e. The Thing) of any true intellect and cunning. Again, while the performances were all solid it ended up being a pretty heartless outing overall and not worthy of its title. My only hope is that people will look to (re)connect with the far superior John Carpenter version. I would love to see more in this universe/sub-genre/whatever but if they are going to throw out the truly interesting and unique aspects, why bother? Judging by the reviews Ican only assume the vast majority enjoy loud, CGI pseudo-gore action/sci-fi/horror films and it is a pity.

B006UG90RM|4.0|It is hard to review this album, and I will admit that whole-heartedly and will explain.  I was 10 in 1984, and I fell head over heals in love with Van Halen at that time.  I was broken hearted when David Lee Roth left the band, but I followed both sides as a listener.  I fully embraced the new VH release of 5150, and I enjoyed it immensely.  I loved Eat Em And Smile as well.  As years went on, both sides failed to release music that held my attention.  I longed for a \\"true\\" reunion of Van Halen.  I did not see Van Halen live with David Lee Roth, but I was able to catch two tours with Sammy Hagar.  The For Unlawful Carnal Knowledge Tour was very good, but the 2004 Hagar reunion was a disappointment.  I dismissed Van Halen altogether for a long time, and I found other things to like and listen to.  However, the old Van Halen albums would be dusted off and listened to with great joy.<br /><br />When it was announced that Roth was going to tour with Van Halen in 2007 without Michael Anthony on bass, I cried \\"Blasphemy!\\"  I plugged my ears and went on humming things to block out the news.  I think Michael Anthony is not only underrated as a bass player, but is really the background vocals in Van Halen regardless of who is the front man.  I thought yeah whatever Wolfie on bass, ok?  Nothing released in terms of new music.  Ugh.  I figured there was no hope of an album or anything substantial ever.  I again was resigned to the fact that Van Halen was essentially history.<br /><br />Then...a single, then an announcement of a tour and an album.  I finally heard the song, \\"Tattoo.\\"  Oh, ah, yeah, I am not sure...the rest of the album is going to be terrible if this is the lead single.  However, I found myself humming the song, and every once in a while singing, \\"Tattoo, tattoo!\\"  It grew on me.  Then I found previews of other songs on the internet.  I recognized that about half of the album was directly off the Gene Simmons demo that I already had found on the internet a few years ago.  I thought this is nonsense...<br /><br />However, I went to my favorite record store and picked up a copy of the deluxe edition.  I watched the DVD of acoustic songs first.  That was somewhat cool to see David Lee Roth actually singing within a few feet of Eddie...funny that they have been apart for so long.  I listened to the album.  It was ok.  I thought the revised versions of demos sounded better on an official release, and the new lyrics dressed up the songs a bit.  Then I played it again.  I loaded on my iPod Nano and listened while running.  I put it in the car stereo and cranked it a bit.  It is growing on me to the point of near obsession.  My favorite band from childhood actually reunited with the lead singer and released an album!!!  Oh my god...it really happened.  First, I will say that Eddie's guitar playing is again inspired and fresh.  It sounds like the creative Eddie that experimented with sounds, pick-ups, etc.  The bass playing (I don't know if the rumors are true that Eddie played the bass in studio) sounds more up front and gives a nod to Billy Sheehan on Eat Em And Smile.  If Wolfie is in fact doing all the bass duties, then he is doing a great job.  There are different tones of distortion and walking bass lines that are enjoyable.  The background vocals sound similar under the father-son duo, but I can tell that Mad Mike is not there.  The drumming is classic Alex.  Nothing stands out on the drums as the opening of Hot For Teacher does on 1984, but this is a solid performance.  Now the best part, David Lee Roth's voice...it sounds surprisingly youthful.  There are a couple of his signature screams in there.  Is it the same as the golden era?  Of course not.  The guy is 56 years old, but he does still have the same style and energy as he did on 1984.  I have always preferred Roth to Hagar as many VH fans out there, and I am happy to have him back to the point of allowing myself to understand he has aged...albeit pretty well.<br /><br />But what about Michael Anthony?  I realized from the acoustic songs on the DVD that half of the background vocals are still there.  Eddie does a lot more background vocals than I realize (as many a VH fan may not always remember).  One might forget that Ed can use his pipes as well as his fingers.  He uses a falsetto style to give that high-pitched VH chorus a hint of yesterday.  MOREOVER, when given the opportunity to bring your son into the family business, who wouldn't?  I know I would love to have that type of involvement with my son later in life.  Do I miss Michael Anthony and feel bad for him?  Sure.  However, I think there is an excitement for a somewhat unknown (Wolfgang) to join his dad on stage and rock!  In addition, I think Michael Anthony is doing ok with Joe Satriani and company in a little thing called Chickenfoot.<br /><br />As far as half the album being old tunes?  Well, when you look at the history of Van Halen with David Lee Roth as singer, you will notice that they rehashed old material every once in a while.  Many of the 25 songs from the Warner Brothers demos were sprinkled over the 6-album discography.  Much of the original Van Halen catalog was written prior to their being signed by a major label and included in their touring in 1977 and 78.  Download the Gene Simmons demo or the Warner Brothers demo and see for yourself.  One example is House Of Pain, which was written and performed early in VH's history, but not recorded until the release of 1984.  Some of the first songs did not make the first record, and many of them made it onto VH II.  The old material provides a type of marriage to the new material tying 1977-78 to 2012.  Thinking of it this way made sense to me in a way that allowed my hackles to go down.<br /><br />As far as this new songs and album goes...I really enjoy listening to it.  I have done so at least two dozen times now, and I really enjoy it.  Due to the length of this review, I will comment on my own review with a track-by-track analysis including a note on which songs are old ones and on the Gene Simmons demo.realized from the acoustic songs on the DVD that half of the background vocals are still there. Van Halen had a chance to be a constant musical force in 1991 when they were on top with the superb \\"carnal knowledge.\\" The band's cowardly move to steer the music into the grungy-whiny-sloppy direction in 95 ultimately ruined them. After the lukewarm reception in their US and European tours, and the public's ignorance of their much hyped trash-metal-esque movie soundtrack song, Eddie got rid of Sammy to reinvent the band. Then, the even whinier-grungier-slopppier VHIII came out. Then the two trash-metalized songs with Sammy in 04. Fans rejected all of that and wanted to hear the fun, fast, and furious Van Halen. The songs lack soul, the performances lack inspiration, the recording lacks direction. Even the album cover is stolen. And don't even get me started with Dave's singing/rambling. Just listen to the actual \\"blood&fire.\\" that is, \\"ripley\\" from 1984, and you hear the kind of chemistry and inspiration that had put Eddie, Al and the first two incarnations of the band to the top of musical excellence. Now, all I can hope for Eddie is that he stays healthy and has a happy personal life, since he already gave us so much greatness.

If only he sang the songs like he did on track 12, the album would have sounded much more pleasant. His pronunciation sounds funny. It's almost as if he's wearing false teeth or something. Perhaps he meant to sound as if he were singing at an intimate night club, piano-bar style. If that was the intent, he failed miserably. Instead, his voice just sounds old and out of place. I think he could lower the key on many tunes, not because he can't reach the high notes but because it would suit this music style much better. Ringo's Sentimental Journey album was a good example. All in all, unless you are a die-hard McCartney fan like myself and MUST have every single one of his albums (again like myself), I'd skip this one and hope that his next album reaches back to his rock'n roll roots.  *** NEW comments ***  After listening to the album quite a few times, I'd like to add a few more comments about it. This time, just about the music.  McCartney is probably one of the best, most versatile composer of our time. Not that everything he's written is good, on the contrary. But, he has certainly composed hundreds of great songs. This album does not go without. \\"Only Our Hearts\\" is a beautiful tune that perfectly blends with all other great old songs he chose. Unless you read the booklet, it's almost impossible to tell that this is a brand new song. Steve's harmonica was perfect and complemented the song beautifully. \\"My Valentine\\" on the other hand sounds ordinary. Not really out of place but not very good either. Now, talking out-of-place, I really did not understand song \\"The Inch Warm\\" is there. Cute tune and all but it has absolutely nothing to do with the mood and style of the album. With a different arrangement, it could easily be on the B side of Mary Had a Little Lamb! I'm sure Paul left out many other tunes that would be better fits.

B000JML7EC|5.0|This is a terrific adventure story. It capitalizes on the romance of piracy, while exposing the moral emptiness in an entertaining way.<br />A story from the point of view of pirates or the ships they attacked would simply be about straight up murder, robbery and rapine. By starting out on dry land, and focusing on the treasure on a tropical island in the Caribbean, Stevenson is able to present the pirates as real, fearsome but pitiable people.<br />Although Jim Hawkins was warned to watch out for the one legged man, he and everyone else falls for the lies of Long John Silver -- one indication people with lost limbs were so not unusual at that time.<br />Long John Silver is capable of absolute moral relativism (in modern terms). He's capable of being pleasant, friendly and seemingly reliable while plotting treachery. When his plans fail (partly because the pirates are incapable of sustained discipline), he switches sides again to sell out his friends and escape the noose, and then slips away at the end.<br />You have to wonder why a fast food chain would be named after him.<br />The author has a keen eye for the fun of a treasure hunt, and even keener eye for the human failings of both the good guys and bad guys.

It's one of the most suspenseful moments in the story. 0679745203|5.0|When a book has won the Booker Prize and the film that it spawned has taken Oscars, the casual reviewer might be tempted to conclude that everything has already been said on its subject. Having just revisited the film after several years of absence, I decided to re-read the book. I don't remember how many times I have read it now: let's call it several. I have seen the film at least six times.<br /><br />First let it be said that the film, The English Patient, claims only to be based on Michael Ondaatje's book. It is a film from the book, not of the book. The distinction is crucial because, despite the film's admirable attempt to recreate the complexity of part of the novel, the book always went much further.<br /><br />In the book we have characters who have been scarred by war, by a war that none of them particularly wanted to fight. I suppose there are occasional wars where some of the participants want to be active. But here Caravaggio just wanted to stay a thief and thus keep his thumbs. And who would take over thieving if he is drafted to fight? Perhaps Hana's father really did intend to see out the conflict and restart his previous life. Perhaps the English Patient, himself, did really want to be English. I doubt it. Or perhaps the idea, that of nationality, given war, was mere irrelevance. It was sides that people counted. He certainly had much to hide, but from whom? What does it matter what side you claim to be on when it is only ever the innocent who fall victim? This last point is crucial to the feelings of Kip, the character who only just makes it into the film.<br /><br />For in the book this Sikh sapper, this bomb disposal specialist, who risks his own life to protect others, is a complex anti-colonial thinker. He has a sense of justice that transcends victory, especially when that victory is won at tremendous cost in the lives of those who did not fight. This aspect the film makers largely ignored. His character became a suspiciously like an aspect of the noble savage that remains gratefully unthreatening to colonialism. In the book his standpoint is far more radical than this.<br /><br />And as far as Almasy is concerned, if that really was his name, he eventually worked for those people who would accept him at face value, without a racism that was suspected. On the other hand, he was Hungarian, and in that war the nation was sympathetic to fascism. So did he merely support his own country's line? Whom would you believe? Whose motives are honest?<br /><br />Almasy's love for the wife of a British war-monger was undoubtedly sincere, but at the same time obsessive. Might it have burned out if given the freedom to flame? And did Katharine know of her husband's contribution to war? If not, who was betrayed? In the film it is unclear that it took Almasy three years to return to the Cave of the Swimmers, and also spent much of the intervening time doing significantly more than merely handing over maps. Such is life in war. In film, it's the gloss that counts.<br /><br />In The English Patient, Michael Ondaatje's book, we are never clear about motives. These change whilst apparently remaining both consistent and sincere, despite remaining unknown, often unstated. B006LR0XLM|5.0|One of the few apps it would definitely be difficult to live without.  First, let's clear the air.  Dropbox is a syncing program.  It is not primarily cloud storage or a file sharing service.  It has those features, too, but that's not its main purpose.  Dropbox is the best syncing program out there, bar none.  There are other programs that appear to do the same thing (box, SafeSync,...) but, as of the time of this review, none of them can measure up to Dropbox--some because they are designed more for file sharing than syncing, others because they simply don't perform as well.  Dropbox will sync all of your devices, automatically.  Copy something into your dropbox folder on one device and it appear on your other devices, too.  * On PCs, the file is physically copied to the hard drive.  * With the Android app, a link appears in the Dropbox folder.  The file is downloaded only if you click on the link.  This is to avoid filling up the limited storage in most Android devices.  Surprise!  The latest beta version of Dropbox has an automatic Camera Upload feature. It's beta, meaning you have to visit the Dropbox website to get it and there are some kinks to be worked out.  However, what it does...take a picture or a video and it's AUTOMATICALLY added to your Dropbox.  How much easier can file transfer get?

0307959856|3.0|With each acquisition of new books I take the one I'm least likely to \\"love\\" or which is overly long and work back to the one I expect will be my favorite. With this one the reviews have not been great. Notwithstanding I finally decided to read it almost out of loyalty. I was, in the final analysis, not only relieved but nearly ecstatic. As you may be aware James is the Grande Dame of mystery or Crime Fiction having won all the right awards and over a career spanning 50 years has provided us with not only good sensible plots and story lines but some of the classiest writing in the genre. My concern here was two fold: 1) She turned 91 this past year, and 2) She submerged an excellent story into a well known literary standard with apologies.  Her age was only a concern because I anticipated that this was a bit of a last hurrah with the fingerprints of a publisher smeared somewhere in the shuffle. I was wrong and after hearing an interview wherein her usual lucidity was on display I took the plunge. On display in her writing is the lyrical Brit language structure we colonists have an appetite for which would make this a pleasure to read even if it had been a recipe for the correct sauce (Bordelaise?) to serve with beef Wellington. You can enjoy that aspect for its own sake.  On the second point let me say that you do not have to read Pride and Prejudice (Austen) first to enjoy and love this offering but while it wouldn't hurt to do so those of you familiar with the story and characters will be glad you did if only because you will feel that you have the secret of the decoder ring. It is unnecessary to say that Pemberley is the Darcy estate which may or may not have been Chatsworth.  The timeframe of the novel is over 200 years ago and hence the usual detective conduct and forensic style investigations have yet to be invented. The methodology however is quite explicit and the members of the \\"upper crust\\" have the approach down pat when it comes to how the matter of murder (if it is murder) must be handled. And of course the trial, abbreviated by our standards, and tweaked down the centuries is surprising in both its speed and brevity. The death of the gentleman occurs just as the main house prepares for a proper Ball and therefore a standard of the old murder mystery where there is a plethora of potential suspects comes into play. This however is not Ten Little Indians. This is a well written, high minded, reasoned and consistently portrayed tale told in alternate (character) straight-line chronology. When matters and conduct come to light you always nod. There aren't any \\"no way\\"s in James' writing. The only untoward criticism might be a little too much \\"wrapping up\\" for my taste. There are a couple sub plots where I might have preferred a little more information but that is pure curiosity and inessential. After all the book is only 300 pages. Despite this, character development is complete, if perhaps subliminal, and the juggling of preexisting personalities to fit the action is expert.  This book is very enjoyable, properly paced and would be ideal for committed book clubs. 3.25* GIBO

I also found that sliding the screwdriver up into it's second position was a little stiff at first but now that I've played with it a bit (aka like 100,000 times) it goes up easily. As to the durability question it is rather durable, thought it must be noted to have the nibs pushed completely in place and their caps securely on tight otherwise you do risk a chance of loosing them.  Now on to the fun stuff. It is most satisfying to point the thing and hear the noise, which is exactly like the tv show. It is a great prop and fun to wave around. I have shown it to my friends who are Dr. Who fans and they also had a good time playing around with it. The same in fact goes for my friends who are not Dr. Who fans, though I did have to spend some time explaining what it was to them. But they know I've been on a Dr. Who kick for awhile and also know I am into that sort of thing. Once they found out about the UV nib they were very excited to try it out.  All in all it was a good purchase. It will take some time to see how durable the thing really is. I hope though I will be sonicing for a long time to come. It is for certain though that more Dr. Who products will be on my Birthday/Christmas lists when the time comes. I am most definitely looking at the Eleventh Doctor's screwdriver for purchase in the future, though perhaps not the near one :)

0385344228|5.0|The buzz on this thriller started nearly a year before the novel was published.  I heard it from so many sources, that it was hard not to believe the hype--which is usually the kiss of death for any book.  What can live up to all that?  Happily, Defending Jacob held up well to the hype.  In the end, it's just a great page-turner, but it was as entertaining as promised.<br /><br />The novel has a framing device.  It opens with a transcript of grand jury testimony between assistant district attorney, Neal Logiudice, and his former colleague, Andy Barber. The transcript reappears all throughout the novel.  Readers quickly learn that Andy also used to work in the DA's office until a case that was tried a year prior.  The case involved the murder of a 14-year-old boy in the town Andy and his family lived in, peaceful, upscale, Newton, MA.  In fact, the victim went to the same junior high school as Andy's son, Jacob.  It is this fact that proves to be Andy's undoing.  As he directs the investigation as the ADA on the case, the crime starts to hit far too close to home, with dire consequences.<br /><br />Framing devices can be incredibly lame.  Here, however, it's used to great effect.  In addition to the transcript's handiness for plot exposition, Landay does a fine job of highlighting the character of these two adversaries through their back and forth.  But more than anything, the deeper I got into the novel, as the two men recounted the investigation and trial that had occurred a year prior, all I could think was:  Why are they rehashing this now?  This after-the-fact conversation ratchets up tension throughout the novel.<br /><br />The author has created an appealing narrator in the form of Andy Barber, and I felt for the hellish situation he and his family found themselves in.  Generally, all of the characters were well-drawn and nuanced.  Landry's use of language was just a bit more interesting than what you see in the typical thriller, describing a news van, for instance, as being \\"barnacled\\" with dishes, boxes, and antennae.  And he can write a nice speech, too, such as this soliloquy from Andy:<br /><br />\\"I do not believe in the court system, at least I do not think it's especially good at finding the truth.  No lawyer does.  We have all seen too many mistakes, too many bad results.  A jury verdict is just a guess--a well-intentioned guess, generally, but you simply cannot tell fact from fiction by taking a vote.  And yet, despite all that, I do believe in the power of the ritual.  I believe in the religious symbolism, the black robes, the marble-columned courthouses like Greek temples.  When we hold a trial, we are saying a mass.  We are praying together to do what is right and to be protected from danger, and that is worth doing whether our prayers are actually heard.\\"<br /><br />In the end, however, thrillers are mostly about plotting and pacing, and on those two counts Mr. Landay shines.  This is one nail-biter of a murder mystery/legal thriller.  And, OMG, what a powerhouse ending!  Eat your heart out, John Grisham!th dishes, boxes, and antennae. 1596064331|4.0|Neal Stephenson's Cryptonomicon is a lengthy historical fiction set during both World War II and the late 1990s with much of the action taking place in the Philippines. In the 1940s, Lawrence Pritchard Waterhouse, colleague of Alan Turing, is hired by the U.S. Navy to help break Axis codes. Meanwhile, Marine Sergeant Bobby Shaftoe, who's too enthusiastic and courageous for his own good, doesn't realize that his troop's job is to make it look like the U.S. hasn't broken the codes, but just happens to always be in the right place at the right time.<br /><br />Waterhouse and Shaftoe know each other only superficially, but their descendants, who've noticeably inherited some of their traits, meet in the 1990s storyline. Randy Lawrence Waterhouse is a systems administrator who's trying to set up an electronic banking system in the Philippines. There he meets Doug and Amy Shaftoe, a father and daughter team who are doing the underwater surveying for Randy's Internet cables. Randy and the Shaftoes eventually realize that they share a secret heritage and together they set out on a massive code-breaking treasure hunt.<br /><br />The plot of Cryptonomicon is clever and elaborate, sometimes exciting (e.g., most scenes with Bobby Shaftoe), frequently funny (such as when Ronald Reagan interviews Bobby Shaftoe, and when the Waterhouse family uses a complicated mathematical algorithm to divide up the family heirlooms), and always informative.<br /><br />Neal Stephenson's fans know (and love) that you can't read one of his books without learning a lot. Predictably, Cryptonomicon is chock full of information. If a character walks past a bank in China, you can bet you're in for a lecture on Chinese banking. If he sees a spider web dripping with dew, you'll be taught how spiders catch their prey. Character backstories are used to teach us about the history of the Jews in Eastern Europe or the familial habits of the Filipinos. In Cryptonomicon there are many pages that think they should be in a textbook on computer circuitry (and some that actually admit they belong in Letters to Penthouse). There are three pages devoted to a doctoral dissertation on facial hair and shaving fetishes, and another three pages of instruction on the proper way to eat Cap'n Crunch.<br /><br />These divergences interrupt the plot and make the book much longer than it needs to be, but you just can't help but forgive Stephenson (or to at least smile and shake your head knowingly as if he has some sort of uncontrollable yet endearing pathology), when you see him poking fun at himself for this very thing. In one scene, Bobby Shaftoe thinks he's in \\"HELL'S DEMO\\" when he's forced to listen to someone \\"explain the organization of the German intelligence hierarchy.\\" Though the lecture causes Shaftoe to hallucinate, the reader still manages to learn something about the Wehrmacht Nachrichten Verbindungen while being thankful to realize that Stephenson knows he has this \\"issue.\\"<br /><br />It's easy to tell that Neal Stephenson loves to do research and loves to impart the knowledge he's gleaned, or ideas he's thought up, and it's hard to criticize him for this, especially since it's all done in his clever, colorful, and entertaining style, even if it's not always relevant to the plot. And sometimes these infodumps can really set a scene. Here's a very short example:<br /><br />\\"The Bletchley girls surround him. They have celebrated the end of their shift by applying lipstick. Wartime lipstick is necessarily cobbled together from whatever tailings and gristle were left over once all of the good stuff was used to coat propeller shafts. A florid and cloying scent is needed to conceal its unspeakable mineral and animal origins. It is the smell of War.\\"<br /><br />Stephenson also delights in creating quirky similes:<br /><br />\\"Like the client of one of your less reputable pufferfish sushi chefs, Randy Waterhouse does not move from his assigned seat for a full ninety minutes...\\"<br /><br />Though I skimmed a few of Stephenson's longer tangents, I was nevertheless entertained by the clever plot of Cryptonomicon. I read the novel in two formats. One was Subterranean Press's signed limited edition which was printed on thick glossy paper and embellished with new artwork by Patrick Arrasmith, several graphs, and even some perl script. My Advanced Review Copy of this book weighs 4 pounds (and it was only paperback -- the published version is hardback). I also listened to MacMillan's audiobook read by William Dufris. I'm sure Cryptonomicon was not an easy book to read out loud, but Dufris did an amazing job, even actually sounding like Ronald Reagan during the Reagan interview.<br /><br />Cryptonomicon won the Locus Award in 2000 and was nominated for both the Hugo and Arthur C. Clarke Awards that year. Pretty big accomplishment for a book that's not even science fiction. For readers who haven't tried one of Neal Stephenson's books yet, Cryptonomicon is a good place to start.d by the clever plot of Cryptonomicon. I read the novel in two formats. One was Subterranean Press's signed limited edition which was printed on thick glossy paper and embellished with new artwork by Patrick Arrasmith, several graphs, and even some perl script. My Advanced Review Copy of this book weighs 4 pounds (and it was only paperback -- the published version is hardback). I also listened to MacMillan's audiobook read by William Dufris. I'm sure Cryptonomicon was not an easy book to read out loud, but Dufris did an amazing job, even actually sounding like Ronald Reagan during the Reagan interview.  Cryptonomicon won the Locus Award in 2000 and was nominated for both the Hugo and Arthur C. Clarke Awards that year. Pretty big accomplishment for a book that's not even science fiction. For readers who haven't tried one of Neal Stephenson's books yet, Cryptonomicon is a good place to start.

0812550706|5.0|Ender Wiggin is a \\"Third.\\" His parents were allowed to have him only because their first two children, Peter and Valentine, showed so much promise. Earth is expecting another Bugger attack from outer space and humans are desperately trying to breed and train the children who they hope will be Earth's saviors. Peter, Valentine, and Ender Wiggin are all geniuses, but Ender seems to have just the right balance of intelligence, resolve, independence, and sensitivity to make a great leader for Earth's international forces.<br /><br />When Ender is only six years old, the International Fleet comes to take him away to Battle School. There he meets dozens of other little geniuses, some who resent Ender for his quick advancement, and some who would even like to get him out of the way. Does Ender have what it takes to make it through the rigorous training and to become the military hero that Earth hopes for? Or, as he fears, will he grow up to be like his tyrannical and sadistic older brother?<br /><br />Ender's Game by Orson Scott Card was published in 1985 and was based on a short story that Card wrote in 1977. The novel won both the Hugo and Nebula Awards. The focus is on Ender's psychological drama as he is raised by indifferent parents (they know he'll be taken away at an early age), tortured by a cruel brother, separated from his beloved sister, and cast without a friend into a highly competitive and often hostile environment where he's expected to rise to the top. During all of this, Ender wonders if he will be responsible for saving Earth, or destroying it. There's a lot resting on Ender's little shoulders and the tension never lets up. At times Ender becomes whiny and emotionally overwrought, but who can blame him?<br /><br />The main characters in Ender's Game are young children and there are plenty of silly jokes about farts and butts, but there's also some bad language, quite a lot of violence, and discussions about diplomacy and politics that will be too heavy for younger readers. Ender's got plenty of personal flaws, too, and he has to deal with some major ethical issues such as does the end justify the means?<br /><br />Thus, Ender's Game is classified as Young Adult. Teens will enjoy Ender's training at Battle School. The trainees are divided into teams and the battle tactics they learn are practiced in mock battles under zero gravity conditions. These scenes are great fun and, surprisingly, they don't become dull because the difficulty and the stakes keep rising. During his time off, Ender plays a fantasy RPG that he wants to conquer without realizing why. He doesn't understand why the game acts as it does, but it turns out to have a provocative significance later on.<br /><br />Ender's Game is fast-paced and becomes steadily more intense as the book goes on. Near the end there's a huge twist that will leave your jaw on the floor, and several other tantalizing discoveries follow. I look forward to reading more about these in the first sequel, Speaker for the Dead, which also won both the Hugo and Nebula awards.<br /><br />Interestingly, the U.S. Marines has Ender's Game on its Professional Reading List, explaining in their Ender's Game Discussion Guide that \\"Ender's Game is more than about the difficulty and excitement that competition provides in preparing for combat. There are lessons in training methodology, leadership, and ethics as well. Such richness in range and treatment has made Card's book an oft-read and re-read title for many years; Ender's Game has been a stalwart item on the Marine Corps Reading List since its inception.\\"<br /><br />I read Audio Renaissance's full-cast audio version of Ender's Game which was narrated mostly by Stefan Rudnicki (my favorite reader), Harlan Ellison, Gabrielle De Cuir, and David Birney. There's also a cameo by Orson Scott Card. This audiobook is terrific. There's also a long but interesting author's note at the end, read by Orson Scott Card. I cannot explain why; maybe it's because the book I was writing at the time seemed like it would benefit itself if I, my book's Writer, would step outside my comfort zone (Crichton, McCarthy, Matheson, Barker) and just try to read The Dark Tower: The Gunslinger. I finally read it because the cover looked sweet, not gonna lie. The ever-binding, story-interweaving Todash Darkness pulled me into the world of King. For instance, the Turtle is in It, and even mentioned in his most recent 11/22/63; and Todash Darkness--that vague conceptual, slightly existentialistic evil that makes a sound like rumbling chimes--which is also mentioned in \\"It\\"; and then there's Randal Flagg, Donald Callahan, Ted Brautigan, the Low Men and the Crimson King: all the former are uniting elements in many Stephen King books. And The Dark Tower series is a gateway drug to his other novels; and his other novels are a gateway drug to The Dark Tower--it's full circle, baby!<br /><br /> But let's talk about The Shining, shall we, because that's why you're reading this. In his introduction for the novel King says that the decision to write (and how to write) The Shining was very crucial in terms of his career as a writer. You must understand that Carrie did not break any rules besides<br /><br /> (this one)<br /><br />  and it was very short, which is ideal for debut novels for any author. It was also original and shocking; another must-have. But Carrie wasn't necessarily epic or long lasting. Then `Salem's Lot was his second. He took risks with style, but nothing else; the pacing was fast, and he didn't get too deep into character history. What you knew about most of the characters were through immediate actions and thoughts. And the town itself was something he was familiar with, along with the people and the professions of the people. It was marketable. It worked. It was filmable.<br /><br /> Then The Shining was written more of a character study about how alcoholism destroys marriages and relationships between fathers and their children. Most of the horror wasn't shown on a golden platter as it was in Carrie and `Salem's Lot; instead, the horror was suggestive. It was there, in the shadows, you could choose to see it or ignore it; it was subtle, but there. Then toward the end, that's when the fireworks begin to go off one after the other . . . but still, for my generation (twenty-somethings) and younger, many will pass up The Shining just because it takes a while to pick up the pace and, hey, there's a movie, too--Jack Nicholson's in it, so they'll just watch that, assuming that they'll get the \\"same thing out of it\\"--which is a shame. Ultimately this was King's attempt to actually write a literarily respectable novel with horror aspects (and he does stay true to horror; it's just not blood and guts all the time).<br /><br /> I won't waste your time with the synopsis; the book is different than the film, but it's not that different and I can't say how it is even if I wanted to (which I don't), because the differences would be spoilers. It's a little scarier than Stanley Kubrick's film adaptation in principles and it had a better story; but the movie is easier to watch. However, if you don't mind a slower paced novel that builds up like tension from a rope being pulled further and further apart until it snaps like a stale jerky, then you should definitely read The Shining--and then make sure you read The Dark Tower series (at least the first four are phenomenal).

Roman Draganesti is the leader of one of the largest vampire covens in the United States. It is no longer necessary for Vampires to feed from humans, allowing the vampire community to become more self-reliant and less dangerous.  Not everyone in the vampire community is happy with this change, however.  There is a group of deadly vampires who believe synthetic blood is an abomination, and those who choose to consume the blood are traitors to their race.  These vampires will use any excuse to start a war with Roman's coven.  One night, during a scientific experiment gone awry, Roman loses one of his fangs.  If he doesn't get it repaired before daylight, the socket will heal and he'll be one-fanged for the rest of his life.  Not willing to risk such an embarrassing situation, Roman turns to Shanna's 24hr (human) dental clinic for the procedure.  Roman shows up only moments before the Russian Mafia - who have hired a vampire assassin to put an end to Shanna's life.  Roman needs a dentist, and Shanna clearly needs rescuing, so Roman saves her life and brings her to his home.  Shanna has no idea that Roman is a vampire at first, nor did she know that such creatures really existed.  When she begins to piece things together, she is torn between her strong feelings for the man who continues to save lives, and the terror she feels at the thought that he isn't human.  Facing their emotions might prove to be more difficult than facing their many enemies.  Review:  I used to stay away from novels that have comedic titles like this one.  I made the assumption that they would be similar to satire movies like Vampires Suck or the Scary Movie franchise, which I don't care for.  Now that I've read a few of them, I've realized that I jumped to the wrong conclusion.  How to Marry a Millionaire Vampire had plenty of humor (especially in the first half) but there was also danger, romance, and an interesting cast of characters.  I really enjoyed it.  There is a clan of men who are Scottish Highlanders AND vampires in Roman's coven.....  how could I not love the combination of my two favorite types of male characters??  Most of the humor in this book came from Shanna's inner dialog, confusion, and irrational behavior.  However, the vampire characters add plenty of humor also.  The difference is that the vampires are very serious creatures, so their humor comes from the serious way they handle situations that are completely ridiculous.  Picture a bunch of vampires trying to pump synthetic blood through a life-like sex doll....  the purpose being to find ways to feed vampires without endangering humans.  Their goal was clearly noble, but the circumstances are absurdly funny.  As much as I liked the book, there were a few weak points.  The humor of the story began to die down in the second half of the book, and it was almost non-existent by the end.  I missed it - it was my favorite part of the book. It also did not provide an actual wedding - merely the suggestion of one.  And, while there is a final confrontation, the book ends with more looming conflict for the cast than what existed before the confrontation.  There was a lot of unfinished business.  I am looking forward to reading more in this series, and I recommend that PR and UF fans give it a shot.  If you think you might be open to a more humorous approach to these genres, then this would be a great place to start.
B00004Y6O9|5.0|Not only was this album one particular bands debut release,but it was also the beginning of a whole new way of looking at hard rock. True with most pop music,very little just comes out of the blues. What was done hear had it's definite origins. But again it was the case of a musician,in this case Eddie Van Halen offering another way the electric rock guitar could be played. Especially in terms of hard rock. As a matter of fact one could almost say this hailed in the whole hair metal genre. And it's interesting to note another viewers comments that this album was part of the \\"disco sucks\\" phenomenon. Of course we all know when the 90's rolled in...well the alternative community went the other route and in turn went after bands like Van Halen and other hair metal pioneers as emphasizing \\"technique over soul\\". Well a lot of music for dancing has much soul. And so do Van Halen. And that,along with everything that makes them a special group is on full display here.<br /><br />  From beginning to end this album is full of a musical and vocal devil may care,rebellious smirk,thanks to the flair of Eddie's playing and David Lee Roth's over the top rock star ego. The grinding \\"Running With The Devil\\" really sets the stage and it goes onto \\"Ain't Talkin' 'Bout Love\\",\\"Jamie's Crying\\" and \\"Feel Your Love Tonight\\". They have Eddie's trademark guitar of course as well as David's vocal personality,as well as the big anthemic choruses and hooks. On the other hand \\"Eruption\\",\\"Atomic Punk\\" and \\"On Fire\\" bring in a ferocious guitar attack and heavy tempo that also anticipates speed metal. Throughout the album there are two factors also involved: extremely adept musicianship and a deep love of the blues almost as much as the spirit of Led Zeppelin. You hear that most of all in their swaggering shuffle of \\"Ice Cream Man\\",beginning actually with an almost acoustic folk/blues intro. On \\"I'm The One\\" they even go into this big band swing/doo wop type chorus,showing genuine affection for the rebel music of bygone era as well as their sentimental side. \\"Little Dreamer\\" has a slower,funkier groove to it and somewhat more polish too.<br /><br />  It's so surprise this band was a protege of Kiss's Gene Simmons,in a somewhat similar manner to the all black rock band Living Color was with Mick Jagger in the 80's. They had the ability to hang onto their own egos AND be paraded about by other established rock stars as well. With all of the powerful guitar solos from Eddie and the often underrated and very adaptable drumming of brother Alex,the one quality that really defines this album aside from rocking out almost completely from song to song is the cleanliness of the production. The sounds of each instrument,vocal harmony and screech are done up with just the right amount echo plex and other studio effects to bring out the theatrics,melody and ability this band always possessed. And while I must say I disagree with the idea that Van Halens first two albums are very similar sounding (I see [[ASIN:B00004Y6O8 Van Halen II]] as a totally different animal than this),this particular album does possess a special quality that gives in the distinction of being among the top 70's rock debut albums.ic of bygone era as well as their sentimental side. \\"Little Dreamer\\" has a slower,funkier groove to it and somewhat more polish too. 145162445X|2.0|Despite the fact that Stephen Hawking's book with Leonard Mlodinow, The Grand<br />Design, begins by intoning, \\"Philosophy is dead,\\" many physicists recently, not<br />least Hawking himself, have ventured to make many controversial philosophical<br />speculations going far beyond the science that is presently well understood.<br />Lawrence Krauss's latest book, A Universe from Nothing: Why There Is Something<br />Rather than Nothing, falls into this category.  Chapters 2-8 are a nice<br />popularization of much of what we have learned in cosmology (modulo a few<br />technical details) and are well worth reading.  (I would have given four stars<br />if the book had just included these chapters and had a reasonable title.)<br />But in my mind as another physicist working in the same general area of cosmology<br />(and perhaps focusing deeper into the quantum aspects of cosmology),<br />its philosophical argumentations fall far wide of the mark of answering<br />the age-old question of why there is something rather than nothing.<br />Krauss essentially redefines the ancient difficult question into different forms<br />that science can address, discusses possible solutions to the restricted questions<br />(themselves highly speculative, as Krauss carefully recognizes), and then seems<br />to imply that these speculative answers to the restricted questions solve<br />the ancient difficult problem.<br /><br />As I would put it, the question of why there is something rather than nothing is<br />the question of why there is anything not logically necessary (something) rather<br />than not anything not logically necessary (nothing).  True theorems of<br />mathematics (the logical following of the conclusions from the axioms, not the<br />axioms or conclusions themselves, which might be neither true nor false, such as<br />the axiom of choice, which can consistently be either assumed or negated) seem<br />to be logically necessary, true in all logically possible worlds, so I am<br />excluding them from being counted as `something,' since otherwise it would seem<br />obvious that these supposed `somethings' must necessarily exist.  However,<br />entities like elementary particles, atoms, molecules, sand grains, rocks, life,<br />sex, people, money, books, book reviews, memes, computers, internets,<br />universities, nations, continents, planets, stars, galaxies, electromagnetic<br />fields, space, time, quantum states, quantum operators, quantum probabilities,<br />particle masses, coupling constants, laws of physics, observations, conscious<br />perceptions (or sentient experiences), and even God in my view (though many<br />disagree) are not, so far as I can see, logically necessary, so they are<br />`somethings' in my sense.  They are somethings whose existence has long been<br />mysterious.  Why do any of these entities exist, if there is no logical<br />requirement that they must?<br /><br />Krauss nicely tells how the laws of physics and the existence of a quantum state<br />with a region of space and time containing an electromagnetic field (or<br />containing the gravitational field of a black hole) can lead to the evolution of<br />the quantum state from having no particles at one time to a quantum state having<br />particles at another time.  If only particles are counted as `something,' so<br />that the situation with `only' the laws of physics, space, time, and an<br />electromagnetic field or black hole but no particles counts as `nothing,' then<br />indeed these are cases in which we now understand scientifically how `something'<br />can arise from `nothing.'  However, it is a very strong restriction of<br />`something' to exclude laws of physics, space, time, and electromagnetic and<br />gravitational fields.<br /><br />Krauss goes on to discuss how it might be possible to obtain a scientific<br />understanding of how a situation even without space and time can lead to a<br />situation with space, time, fields, and particles, though again he implicitly<br />assumes some laws of physics and a suitable quantum state.  This case has a<br />subtlety that Krauss does not discuss, in that if one considers a situation<br />without time, what is meant by saying that another situation with time arises<br />out of it?  It can hardly be by the time evolution that was assumed in the case<br />of existing space and time with fields that produce particles at a later time<br />that may not exist at an earlier time.<br /><br />One view of quantum gravity, particularly in what is called canonical quantum<br />gravity (though it has problems not fully understood), is that different times<br />are different components of the quantum state (which may also have components<br />with no time, and perhaps even with no time and no space).  In this view in<br />which time is rather emergent (part of some components of the quantum state,<br />like wetness for water molecules that might not be considered to exhibit this<br />property when in isolation), time is not fundamental, so there is no evolution<br />from one component of the quantum state to another, and certainly not from a<br />component without time to a component with time.  Then one would just say that<br />the full quantum state has some components without time, and other components<br />with time.  So even if one chose not to count the quantum state as `something,'<br />one could hardly say that time arose in the sense of time evolution from<br />non-time, but rather simply that both components without time and components<br />with time exist within the full quantum state.  Without time and time evolution,<br />it also appears to be an oxymoron to say, as the title of Chapter 10, that<br />\\"Nothing is Unstable.\\"<br /><br />Now it might be true (though we certainly do not know yet) that unknown laws of<br />physics, including both the dynamical laws (how things evolve with time if there<br />is time, or more generally in quantum theory, the algebra of quantum operators)<br />and the boundary conditions (which solution of the dynamical laws describes our<br />universe or multiverse, or which quantum state it has if quantum theory is<br />indeed correct), determine that parts (components) of the quantum state have no<br />time or space or particles, and that other parts do have time and space and<br />particles.  Restricting `somethings' to, say, time, space, and particles, one<br />might say that the complete quantum state has both `nothing' and `something,'<br />but even with this restricted sense of `something,' it is rather a strained<br />interpretation to say that the `something' part arose out of the `nothing' part;<br />both simply exist within the total reality.  In any case, the laws of physics<br />(both the dynamical laws and the quantum state) are here being implicitly<br />assumed to exist, and it certainly seems unfair to dismiss them as `nothing.'<br /><br />Krauss goes on to speculate that \\"there may be no fundamental theory at all\\"<br />(page 177), perhaps that there are not even fundamental laws of physics, but<br />that the laws we deduce arise from a situation without laws.  This is certainly<br />speculation (though I do not want to imply that Krauss is suggesting that it is<br />not speculation) going far beyond present science, since by what meta-laws or<br />processes could laws arise from non-laws?  Krauss seems to have in mind current<br />ideas of the multiverse, in which the entirety of our physical reality (let me<br />call it the universe, even if it is a multiverse) has highly varied parts, say<br />with different particle content, particle masses, and coupling constants that<br />might be considered to be part of local `laws of physics' or `bylaws' for that<br />part of the universe.  It is an interesting question, now being addressed within<br />superstring/M theory and its apparent string landscape, how an overall<br />superstring/M theory (not yet fully known) might lead todifferent parts of the<br />universe (pocket universes or sub-universes) having different local bylaws of<br />physics.  In this way the local bylaws might indeed arise from the global laws<br />for the entire universe.<br /><br />But so far there is no convincing evidence I am aware of that there are not<br />global laws for the entire universe or multiverse, even if these laws do not<br />lead to unique laws for the sub-universes.  I see the scientific attractiveness<br />of the multiverse hypothesis as arising from the idea that the laws for the<br />entire multiverse might be simpler and more elegant than the laws for our<br />individual sub-universe, not that there are no laws at all for the entirety.<br /><br />In his preface, Krauss admits that philosophers and theologians have objected to<br />his meaning of `nothing' and claim that he does not understand it.  Krauss's<br />initial response is to make the gratuitous ad hominem reply, \\"I am tempted to<br />retort here that theologians are experts at nothing.\\"  He then says that for<br />them, \\"Nothing is `nonbeing,' in some vague and ill-defined sense.\\"  Well, even<br />though I am a scientist rather than a philosopher or theologian, on this issue I<br />agree with them and think that the idea of nothing as the absence of anything<br />not logically necessary is much more precise and well-defined than Krauss's<br />imprecise ideas of `nothing,' such as \\"the absence of space and time itself\\".<br />(If space and time are emergent properties, how does one define precisely their<br />absence?  How can one define precisely the absence of emergent approximate<br />properties like wetness?)<br /><br />Although I have focused on the philosophical ideas of nothingness that Krauss<br />does seems to engage with more by ridicule than by understanding, I also<br />disagree with his anti-theistic remarks, such as his referring on page 173 to \\"a<br />remarkable all-powerful entity for which there is simply no other evidence.\\"  In<br />contrast to this, the existence of an omnipotent God seems to me to be the<br />simplest explanation for the strong historical evidence for the resurrection of<br />Jesus, for example as recently summarized by the masterful historical treatment<br />by N. T. Wright in his 817-page analysis, The Resurrection of the Son of God.<br />So what Krauss wrote here is a gross overstatement.<br /><br />Now my objection to this error should not be mis-interpreted to be an objection<br />to honest doubt and skepticism, which are indeed helpful in both science and<br />religion in getting closer to the truth, and I do appreciate other places in the<br />book where Krauss seems more honest in his skepticism and less dogmatic about<br />lack of contrary evidence.  If the phrase I quoted had been written something<br />like the following, I would not have objected:  \\"a remarkable all-powerful<br />entity for which I personally do not see convincing evidence.\\"  But to say, in<br />the face of the massive historical evidence that Jesus' disciples believed that<br />they had witnessed his empty tomb and personally had seen him resurrected from<br />death, that there is no other evidence for God, sounds woefully ignorant of the<br />facts.  I personally would advocate skepticism toward unsubstantiated beliefs<br />that \\"there is simply no other evidence\\" for God.<br /><br />In his final paragraph of his Afterward, Richard Dawkins makes the prematurely<br />triumphalist statement, \\"And now we can read Lawrence Krauss for what looks to<br />me like the knockout blow.\\"  To me as a fellow scientist, it appears Krauss has<br />instead swung far wide of the goal, striking only the air with his philosophical<br />speculations that do not address the truly deep questions of existence.<br /><br />Don N. Page<br />Professor of (theoretical gravitational) Physics<br />University of Alberta<br />profdonpage@gmail.coma of quantum operators)<br />and the boundary conditions (which solution of the dynamical laws describes our<br />universe or multiverse, or which quantum state it has if quantum theory is<br />indeed correct), determine that parts (components) of the quantum state have no<br />time or space or particles, and that other parts do have time and space and<br />particles.
