B006UG90RM|5.0|I've been a Van Halen fan for as long as I can remember: As a 10 year old, my favorite song was \\"Little Guitars,\\" and I've never looked back. In junior high, I was devastated when I learned of David Lee Roth's departure, but begrudgingly gave Sammy Hagar a chance. Whether it was due to the changing sound of rock in the 80s, or just my own ability to adapt, Hagar grew on me, and Van Halen continued to be my favorite band. That said, I always knew that this \\"new\\" Van Halen was a very different band: Not worse, not better--just different than the band I fell in love with.  28 years later, that band is back with a vengeance. \\"A Different Kind of Truth\\" solidly rocks from top to bottom -- the sound falling squarely in the \\"Van Halen 2\\" / \\"Women and Children First\\" era. Roth's voice may not have the acrobatic skills of the past, and Michael Anthony is surely missed, but this is classic Van Halen to be sure. Ironically, the weakest track on the album is the first released single (a tactic used in the past by many a band). The rest of the album blazes at a breakneck pace, with the band barely taking enough time to catch their collective breath.  Side note: Much has been made of the band using older, unreleased demos as the basis for *some* of these tracks. News flash: This is a practice employed by every band since the dawn of time. Most prolific musicians have libraries upon libraries of tracks they've stored away for safekeeping, and Eddie Van Halen is no different. In fact, many songs from the Hagar era were written before Sammy even joined the band (\\"Right Now,\\" \\"Mine all Mine,\\" etc.). If anything, the use of older demos has bridged the gap seamlessly to this new, yet classic sound.  Fans of Van Halen will not be disappointed. Fans of real rock and roll in general should thank Van Halen for saving them from the otherwise dreary, vanilla 2012 rock landscape populated by the likes of Nickelback. It might not be the perfect album, but as a comeback for the *true* mighty Van Halen, it works just fine.  I haven't been this excited for a new album release in years. Thanks, Van Halen, for making me feel like that kid in junior high running to my record store--if only for 50 minutes.

1400052181|5.0|This book is the culmination of a 10-year labor of research and discovery, courage and truth-seeking, persistence and perseverance,  love and trust. Rebecca Skloot and Deborah Lacks (Henrietta's daughter) embarked upon a courageous journey to uncover the story of Henrietta Lacks and her tumor cells, called HeLa by those who cultured them. It is a haunting story of the evils of racism, unethical medical practices, and the multiple traumas suffered by the Lacks family. It raises provocative questions that are yet to be answered regarding the ethics of biomedical research and the ownership of human tissues.  My emotions were all over the place while reading this book. Descriptions of inhumane research practices turned my stomach, such as the tests performed on Elsie and other patients in the \\"Crownsville Hospital for the Negro Insane\\" and the experimentation with syphilis on black men and prisoners at Tuskegee. I cringed at the lab practices of the 1950's (e.g., doctors and technicians carried tubes of radium in their pockets and lab techs ate lunch at the same tables where they handled biohazardous materials). I was horrified that the Lacks family could not afford to see a doctor for serious illnesses while biotech companies made millions from Henrietta's cells. My heart soared when Skloot finished the book after so many years. My eyes blurred with tears when Deborah died.  Skloot masterfully interwove a family's true story with interviews from the medical staff at Johns Hopkins, Tuskegee Institute, the NIH and other institutions. She took great care to tell both sides of the story.  But most of all, the affection and respect Skloot and the Lacks had for each other shines through these pages. Thank you, Rebecca Skloot, for telling this compassionate, informative, and transforming story of Henrietta Lacks, the woman behind HeLa.

Also, why was Katniss unconscious for every major event? We are supposed to experience the story through her point of view, not hear about it from her point of view. This also hurt the story. And the final thing, of course, is the way the love triangle played out. Although I thought Gale made more sense, I can understand why she would end up with Peeta, if the right reasons were given. Killing off Prim with a bomb that Gale designed and having Katniss blame him for it was just a cheap and easy way to resolve it(Prim didn't even have to die. Just the fact that Coin killed those children and wanted to stage another Hunger Games would have been enough for Katniss to kill her). But even then, Katniss was longing for Gale back home in 12, hoping he would appear silently in the woods as he always does. But why did she chose Peeta? Simply because he was there, and Gale wasn't. The only thing that made sense in this regard is the one line about Gale being to similar to Katniss, and Katniss needing Peeta to basically balance her out. This should have been elaborated, and it should have been her decision. Instead, she just didn't really care, took what was given. She was a shell of her former self, dead inside. I understand the point that Collins is trying to make about war, but she doesn't even think of resilience or the hope that Katniss always portrayed. Just plain misery, no balance, just misery. She ruined the character of Katniss, and really Gale and Peeta too, just because she had to force her anti-war message down our throats. She butchered the ending of this series, and it's a shame, because the overall message could have been about a whole lot more than misery.

B002MQYOFW|3.0|Perhaps it is the hype, perhaps I just don't get it. It took me most of the book before I realized why I did not like the writing: complete lack of figurative language. No metaphors, no similes, no analogies. For a book that tries so hard to immerse you into its very different world, I found this very disconnecting.  There is very little show, but a lot of tell. The story is first person present tense. Everything is from Katniss' perspective, where she tells you what she is feeling, what she sees, what she is doing, and what she is thinking. The problem is she tells you she is sad, or hungry, or scared. So I know she feels this, but I don't. There is very little description, no way for me to experience her world. What there is a lot of, however, is her thoughts: lots of rambling. While this makes sense: she is hunted and fear for her life in a very strange setting. It gets very, very tiring after a hundred pages. Yet this progresses throughout the entire book. If you can relate to a 14 year old girl, perhaps this will make more sense to you. But I obviously can't (not for the entire duration of the novel anyway), so this felt incredibly annoying. So there is no respite from the endless torrent of her inane thoughts. No other perspective from other characters.  And then there is the problem of character: Katniss has no personality. She is generically a good character. She is skilled at hunting, and she has a good heart. Whoop-dee-freaking-doo. Her main struggle is to stay alive, fake a relationship with the other guy from her district, and stay a live, and stay alive. There is no difficult moral dilemma to confront her. Every friendly person she encounters in the game that might pose such a problem is dealt with in some morally convenient manner. In short, she makes almost no real progression in her character. No development. She only begins to fall for the other guy from her district, Peeta, near the end, and he is but a one dimensional character even less fleshed out than Katniss.  Not even the basic premise worked for me: basically Battle Royale between strangers. While I had hoped since this was focused on just one character it would evoke more emotion, I was wrong. But what gets me is this: a dystopia where children are forced into gladiator style game and the districts are forced to watch, yet there is such a strong need to make it entertaining? If it is some form of punishment, why make it entertaining? I know this somehow makes sense within the context of the story, but I never thought it  believable. Maybe I am just too old for this.  This wouldn't be so bad if Katniss wasn't so thick-headed to begin with. Quite a few \\"surprises\\" were quite obvious from the beginning. But if you are new to pop stories fit for mass consumption (Hollywood, I'm looking at you) then you should find this entertaining.  While this book isn't for me, I realize I am in a very tiny minority. I just wanted to voice my opinion in case someone else finds it useful.

B002XZNHG4|5.0|It's easy to forget, with all its accessibility and charm, how groundbreaking Annie Hall was. Like Altman's Nashville, Annie Hall seemed to take every cinematic convention of the time and turn it upside down, or rear end it, or transform what it's usually used for for completely different ends: cartoons, flashbacks, voice-over narration from two points of view, breaking the fourth wall, monologues that come off like modern Shakespearean soliloquies, and more. The \\"plot\\" is bare bones. Two relatively normal people share a romance and live to tell about it. Annie Hall is that tale, but the seeming simplicity is made complex by smarter than smart dialogue, obscure references, and fantastic performances, especially by Diane Keaton who LIVED the part of Annie so much that it's difficult to tell where the actress left off and the real life personality began. From beginning to end, Annie Hall is a delight. Brilliant editing, creating a seamless through-line out of what must have been an editing room mess-unless Woody story-boarded every scene; a production design that makes the seventies seem not one bit dated, and hilarious dialogue with gags that get funnier with each viewing. This was my first foray as a young man into what I'd call \\"smart\\" cinema, where the director and writer don't talk down to you but instead make you do some work to keep up with the characters. Woody's unsurpassed masterpiece.

B005I4JG80|5.0|This is a fun and entertaining read. I found myself laughing out loud more than once and finishing the book in less than 24 hours. She gives an honest account of how these ideas have worked with her own children and where she's had difficulty or been uncomfortable, while at the same time trying to fully understand and communicate the subtly different philosophy of parents she's encountered in France.

B002GHHHKQ|5.0|(contains spoilers)<br /><br />In my review I don't wish to say too many things about the technical aspects of the movie. Many of the reviewers seem frustrated by the, what they consider, ambiguity the movie holds towards this question, which they feel is significantly different than the balance in the book. I have a very different opinion about this.<br /><br />I am, I should say, completely atheist. I am also a social scientist. This movie, in my opinion is a one of a kind social science fiction movie. The contact with extraterrestrial beings is the premise, but the real story is that of human society and the reaction to the contact. Sagan, atheist himself, devoted much of his life to try to educate people about science, and about understanding religion from a social history perspective, not as a relevant tool to understand the material world today. In Cosmos, Sagan explores the reactions of human society and social structures to a possible contact. Unfortunately, we still live in a very backwards, religious society, and this is what the movie shows. The focus on religious dilemmas in light of the first contact is unfortunately very likely to happen in such case. The impossible position that Foster's character finds herself at the end does not leave the \\"science vs religion\\" debate open handed, as we learn that the evidence which proves her main argument (that she experienced 14 hours in what seemed like a fraction of a second), and shows how it is being hushed by the government.<br /><br />In another book, Sagan wrote of the \\"demon-haunted world\\", and traced the struggles of scientists to stand by the evidence in a repressively religious environment. Contact, the book and the movie, puts forth an argument that today, in many ways, we still live in a demon-haunted world, in which the role and social power of religion is much too strong.

Now, if you loved that 1st album and loved the Hard Rock music genre you'll remember what else you were listening to back then? UFO, Aerosmith, Black Sabbath, Blue Oyster Cult, Scorpions, Rush etc... To us growing up back then, we liked all this music in this genre, including Sammy Hagar.<br /><br />Now, I do agree, Van Halen is NOT Van Halen without David Lee Roth. Pure and simple. I'm SO glad for Spotify, which allows me to preview the whole CD without having to buy it.<br /><br />I read one review that said something to the effect of: \\"How can a TRUE Van Halen fan dislike this album?\\" and also, \\"You're not a fan if you don't like this album.\\" Well, that's BS!<br /><br />I really think that people who pre-ordered this CD or bought the digital MP3 version, had SO much faith vested in it, that they were going to like it no matter what. B006W0QOF2|5.0|I was expecting DRIVE to be another car-chase extravaganza ala FAST AND FURIOUS---boy was I wrong--and pleasantly surprised.<br />DRIVE is a complex character study with a few hot car chases--and it's also a strangely sentimental love story.  Director Nicholas Winding Refn effectively uses scenes of silence instead of cliched dialogue to illustrate the love story; subtle changes in lighting; a retro-sounding musical score that was actually written between the years 2007-2011.<br />The script avoids many standard cliches regarding stereotyped ex-cons, mobsters, villains, etc.  The Driver's anonymity and his abrupt comfort with violence makes his character intriguing. It is an intelligent, original action movie with substance.  Highly recommended.

1439192812|5.0|I've always been a huge Richard Dawkins fan: The Selfish Gene, The River Out of Eden, The Ancestor's Tale, and on, and on, and on.<br /><br />This is a guy whose produced one incredibly thought provoking book after another.<br /><br />But here, writing for younger readers, Dawkins is perhaps at his best, breaking down difficult scientific concepts for a younger audience.<br /><br />In this wonderful little book, you'll learn about everything from where life came from to what rainbows are really made of.<br /><br />In a breezy almost stream of thought sort of way, Dawkins moves easily from one topic to another, always staying long enough to tell the story of what myth has had to say on the subject juxtaposed with what science now tells us.<br /><br />In this way, readers not only learn (in plain, simple English) what science does have to say as to these issues but also the important lesson that -- whatever our ancestor's ancestor's believed -- we now have the technology and the power to find out the real answers about why things are the way they are.<br /><br />For parents interested in helping nudge their kids in the direction of healthy -- non myth based -- understanding of the world around them I would happily recommend this book along with J. Richard Gott's equally wonderful Sizing up the Universe and a subscription to Jr. Skeptic as well.<br /><br />For the good or bad of it, the quality of life our kids will have is all too directly connected with the type of questions they themselves are willing to ask of themselves, others, and nature itself.

B0053VKP8S|5.0|This little gizmo was pretty much plug-and-play from the SPDIF output on my M-Audio Audiophile 2496 sound card. Just connect it to the digital output with a suitable cable, set the switch to either Optical or SPDIF, and the audio appears at the analog outputs on the FiiO d3. No settings to program or software to install. Nice.<br /><br />  The quality of the sound is indistinguishable from the analog outputs on the same card, which is a $100.00 entry-level musician's sound card. So while a true audiophile might find this lacking, it's probably more than adequate to your needs if you're seriously considering a D/A converter in this price range to begin with.<br /><br />  By the way - for the first several weeks, whenever I STOPPED playing audio from the computer for more than a few minutes, the D3 would spit out horrible bursts of digital noise. After much fiddling with my software settings, I found that this behavior went away when I set the sound card to 16-bit/44,100Hz. The D3 is advertised as being compatible with 192KHz/24 Bit audio, so this was a bit of a disappointment, but it still sounds totally acceptable even at the lower settings. I am very happy with the purchase overall.

There was so much potential here to really make use of these photographs, but Riggs failed to use these awesome discoveries in any unique or interesting way.  If you enjoy science-fiction and fantasy novels in general, then by all means, buy and enjoy this book.  But if you are looking for a creepy, haunting ghost story, do not expect much.  After the first few chapters, you will be disappointed.  It's really not a terrible book, I just think the cover and description are misleading.  The pictures themselves, because they are real, vintage photographs, are worth looking at (I finished the book several days ago but still go back and flip through the pictures). I also think it's really neat that these photos came from collections of real people who discovered them in garage sales and thrift stores--it makes them that much cooler and more interesting. But the story really seemed to veer off track about halfway through the book and the it seemed that Riggs tried too hard to make the pictures fit the story.  Instead of having the pictures add to the story, I think the story actually took away from the pictures.  Bottom line of my opinion: pictures are fascinating that almost, in and of themselves, make the book worth checking out, but I think the cover and description are misleading. Not terrible, but not what I was expecting and didn't ever reach its potential. Also-I HATE when stories end on a cliffhanger with absolutely no satisfiable resolution.

So reading it with fresh eyes, I found that I really enjoyed Delirium.<br /><br />It didn't invoke the crazed fangirl in me, but Delirium took an interesting look at a society that views love as a disease.<br /><br />That aspect of the novel really threw me at first. I mean, who would ever really want to remove the capability to love others because they thought it was unhealthy and the root of all evil?! But, as I kept reading, I understood more and more that making people stop loving was a surface issue and the real problems ran much deeper than that. I really liked that about Delirium - it teases with you with an idea then panned out and shows you the huge scope of what's really going on.<br /><br />Character Relationships: Lena and Alex. Alex and Lena. Being completely honest, I didn't get them at first. Lena is brainwashed to believe and trust that love is dangerous, and Alex is this rebel who she meets at a party. She's intrigued by him, and I understand the attraction, but I don't see it on his side.<br /><br />Why does he like her? I never really got a sense of why he's attracted to Lena. This could definitely just be me, though. I need to find out more because it's killing me. I loved their very realistic relationship, and I hope we see more from Hana in Pandemonium (out February 28!).<br /><br />OVERALL:<br /><br />Delirium is an interesting story with well-developed characters and a killer cliffhanger. I definitely want to read more from Oliver, and I'm anxiously waiting for Pandemonium to answer some serious questions. 0060899220|4.0|If you've seen Anthony Bourdain's show No Reservations, you'll instantly recognize his style. It's sort of a \\"I'll say exactly what I think\\" attitude that often comes across as witty, funny, and honest, yet sometimes it comes across as brash, arrogant, and obnoxious. Either way, it's difficult to deny that Bourdain is a great writer.  I first heard Kitchen Confidential described as sort of an expose on the restaurant industry, but that's hardly an accurate description. Sure, it describes why ordering a well-done steak will get you the worst piece of meat they have, why restuarants might recycle untouched bread baskets, and why chefs often try to push older product to customers, but this is a pretty small section of the book (less than 15 pages). Frankly, I found none of these things either shocking or disturbing.  The book is mostly about the experience of a professional kitchen. Bourdain talks about getting into the business, the characters therein, and stories that he has accumulated over the years. Many of these stories were both interesting and genuinely hilarious, however, there were definitely sections I found a bit boring. Bourdain makes it clear that this book was written mostly for his fellow cooks, and I can imagine that they (or anybody else in the restaurant business) would get much more out of it than I did.  Overall, I found that the book is taken best in small doses. It's entertaining enough to plow through in two or three days, but too much of Bourdain at once leaves a bad impression, like working with the guy must be nightmarish experience. Whether or not that's true, Kitchen Confidential is still a pretty good book.
